    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer2594144992074979582">Life at the court of Louis XVI and Marie Antoinette has long captivated readers, drawn by accounts of the intrigues and pageantry that came to such a sudden and unexpected end. Stefan Zweig's <i>Marie Antoinette: The Portrait of an Average Woman</i> is a dramatic account of the guillotine's most famous victim, from the time when as a fourteen-year-old she took Versailles by storm</span>
  <span id="freeText2594144992074979582" style="display:none">Life at the court of Louis XVI and Marie Antoinette has long captivated readers, drawn by accounts of the intrigues and pageantry that came to such a sudden and unexpected end. Stefan Zweig's <i>Marie Antoinette: The Portrait of an Average Woman</i> is a dramatic account of the guillotine's most famous victim, from the time when as a fourteen-year-old she took Versailles by storm, to her frustrations with her aloof husband, her passionate love affair with the Swedish Count von Fersen, and ultimately to the chaos of the French Revolution and the savagery of the Terror. An impassioned narrative, Zweig's biography focuses on the human emotions of the participants and victims of the French Revolution, making it both an engrossingly compelling read and a sweeping and informative history.<br />"Certainly no one can arise unmoved from the reading of this powerful work." -- <i>The New Republic</i><br />"Excellent biography." -- <i>The New York Times</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="2594144992074979582" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/787961-can-anyone-tell-me-what-approach-the">Can anyone tell me what approach the author takes in the matter of the relationship between Marie Antoinette and Count Fersen? Does he state that they truly had an affair or claims it was just platonic love?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_787961'><a id="like_count_community_question_787961" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/787961?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">3 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F59146.Marie_Antoinette">like</a></span>

</li>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/2384784-lydia"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1509295613i/2384784._UY100_CR16,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/2384784-lydia">Lydia</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I believe that they had an affair. It might have started as platonic, but after her marriage was consummated, there was opportunity to advance an affa<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I believe that they had an affair. It might have started as platonic, but after her marriage was consummated, there was opportunity to advance an affair. I stated in my review after the first child was born that was really when the affair came to fruition. A lot depended on the intimacy of Marie Antoinette and Louis XVI, of which I think that there was little.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1020874439" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1020874439" />
    <a title="Teresa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/541416-teresa"><img alt="Teresa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1570841350p2/541416.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1020874439?book_show_action=true">Aug 08, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Teresa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Teresa" href="/user/show/541416-teresa">Teresa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1020874439" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8030834868591938330">I've now read three different types of books by Zweig: a novella (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59151.Chess_Story' title='Chess Story by Stefan Zweig' rel='nofollow noopener'>Chess Story</a>), a memoir (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/629429.The_World_of_Yesterday' title='The World of Yesterday by Stefan Zweig' rel='nofollow noopener'>The World of Yesterday</a>) and this biography. Though I'm more likely to read another of his novellas instead of another of his biographies, against expectation, I found this the most entertaining of the three books, but also the most problematic.<br/><br/>It's written in a very engaging style (though with too many exclamation points for my taste) and I zipped through it as I neared the end. Unlike with his memoir, in w</span>
  <span id="freeText8030834868591938330" style="display:none">I've now read three different types of books by Zweig: a novella (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59151.Chess_Story" title="Chess Story by Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">Chess Story</a>), a memoir (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/629429.The_World_of_Yesterday" title="The World of Yesterday by Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">The World of Yesterday</a>) and this biography. Though I'm more likely to read another of his novellas instead of another of his biographies, against expectation, I found this the most entertaining of the three books, but also the most problematic.<br /><br />It's written in a very engaging style (though with too many exclamation points for my taste) and I zipped through it as I neared the end. Unlike with his memoir, in which there is an unusual distance between the writer and the reader -- again contradicting expectation -- there is no distance here. Zweig is upfront in his opinions and self-assured in his psychological dissection of Marie Antoinette and her contemporaries. Phrases like <i>girlish trivialities</i>, <i>the premonition of a true sexual awakening</i> and <i>the tender emotion</i> read as blanket statements. At the first mention of Marie Antoinette's letters to Count von Fersen, Zweig concludes with this extraordinary (for him in relation to his subject, that is) sentence: <i>These letters modify our whole outlook upon the character of a woman hitherto regarded as light-minded.</i> and I laughed out loud. <br /><br />Before the Revolution, Zweig portrays the Queen as unseeing and frivolous, never malicious, and the king as unseeing and ineffective, both focusing on their private lives and practically nothing on the issues of the day. Though Zweig seems to respect Marie Antoinette more as time goes on, if she is what he considers <i>an average woman</i> (as his subtitle and some of his comments state) I feel demeaned.<br /><br />Zweig excels at synthesizing vast amounts of information and details. His storytelling skills shine, so much so that certain passages, such as the thoughts he puts in Marie Antoinette's head, especially near the end of her life, read as historical fiction. He writes of regrets in the <i>secret hearts</i> of most of the deputies who had voted to execute the king. He talks of the sentries hiding <i>the sympathy they felt for the prisoners</i> despite what <i>the royalist memoirs tell us</i>, but says nothing of what leads him to his own conclusion of the <i>common folk</i>'s solicitude. Wondering several times where his information came from, I became frustrated. In his afterword he explains what sources he has <b>not</b> used, and why.<br /><br />Occasionally he assumes knowledge from his readership that no longer commonly exists. Translation is not always provided, not much of an issue except in the cases of lyrics of scurrilous songs and the lengthier passages from letters. That is not a criticism but an observation on how writing and reading has changed from Zweig's time.<br /><br />Alain Robbe-Grillet is famous for saying that psychology was the worst thing to happen to the novel (I recently read that paraphrase in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18114449.An_Unnecessary_Woman" title="An Unnecessary Woman by Rabih Alameddine" rel="nofollow noopener">An Unnecessary Woman</a>), but I'm positive Zweig believed it to be the best thing that happened to any kind of writing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8030834868591938330" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_994360741" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/994360741" />
    <a title="Sue" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3642045-sue"><img alt="Sue" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274829293p2/3642045.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/994360741?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sue" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sue" href="/user/show/3642045-sue">Sue</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/6675946-marie-antoinette">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=library-book">library-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=austria">austria</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=bio-memoir">bio-memoir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=read-2014">read-2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=france">france</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer994360741" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1267111406282120295">The writings of Stefan Zweig, who died over 70 years ago, are now enjoying a sort of resurgence as they are being published again and are being introduced to new generations of readers. Personally, I have now read four of his works this year including this biography, his autobiography, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/629429.The_World_of_Yesterday' title='The World of Yesterday by Stefan Zweig' rel='nofollow noopener'>The World of Yesterday</a>, and two works of fiction, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59151.Chess_Story' title='Chess Story by Stefan Zweig' rel='nofollow noopener'>Chess Story</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/224787.Fantastic_Night___Other_Stories' title='Fantastic Night & Other Stories by Stefan Zweig' rel='nofollow noopener'>Fantastic Night & Other Stories</a>. In all these works, Zweig proves himself to be an excellent storyteller. He can write well and he has made the lif</span>
  <span id="freeText1267111406282120295" style="display:none">The writings of Stefan Zweig, who died over 70 years ago, are now enjoying a sort of resurgence as they are being published again and are being introduced to new generations of readers. Personally, I have now read four of his works this year including this biography, his autobiography, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/629429.The_World_of_Yesterday" title="The World of Yesterday by Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">The World of Yesterday</a>, and two works of fiction, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59151.Chess_Story" title="Chess Story by Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">Chess Story</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/224787.Fantastic_Night___Other_Stories" title="Fantastic Night &amp; Other Stories by Stefan Zweig" rel="nofollow noopener">Fantastic Night &amp; Other Stories</a>. In all these works, Zweig proves himself to be an excellent storyteller. He can write well and he has made the life of Marie Antoinette very interesting to one who is not at all expert in French history.<br /><br />The early years of her life as Queen are not dealt with kindly but then it does not appear that there was much to be kind about. <i>Noblesse oblige</i> ruled the day with the royalty of France and MA certainly participated in that rule.<br /><br /><br /> <i>She liked to buzz hither and thither, to be always <br /> beginning some new task which she would never finish, to<br /> be continually occupied without any serious exertion; she<br /> loved to feel that time was not standing still with her, <br /> or that she was out-running time. To be quick at her meals,<br /> content perhaps with eating a sweetmeat or two; to sleep <br /> only for a short time, never to think long, to be perpetually<br /> on the go, frittering away her days---such were her desires. <br /> the twenty years, or rather less, during which Marie <br /> Antoinette was Queen were characterized by an unceasing <br /> movement in an orbit around her own ego. Since, outside <br /> this orbit, she had no goal, nor any inward conviction of<br /> an an aim, from the human and political standpoint she was<br /> circling in the void.</i> (p 81)<br /><br /><br />As for the contrast between the king and queen:<br /><br /><br /> <i>He was heavy, she was light; he was inert, she was mobile; <br /> he was dull, she was sparkling; his nerves were obtuse, hers<br /> were sensitive....He was irresolute, she was quick to make<br /> up her mind;...he was strictly religious, she was in love<br /> with things of this world; he was modest and unassuming, she<br /> was coquettishly self-assertive; he was pedantic, she was <br /> frivolous; he was thrifty, she was extravagant....</i><br /> (pp 82-83)<br /><br /><br />These character points would eventually come home to haunt the Queen, though she likely did not understand why. There are amazing descriptions of her spending excesses, all paid for by the people of France who were suffering.<br /><br />Only twenty years later, after the maternal warnings of Maria Theresa in writing from Vienna or through her helpers went unheeded, did MA change when the Revolution forced change upon her. <br /><br /><br /> <i>"Tribulation first makes one realize what one is"---<br /> this touching phrase, a sign that the writer had been <br /> profoundly shaken, now appeared in one of her letters. <br /> For two decades no warnings, not those of her mother nor<br /> those of her friends, had any effect upon this defiant<br /> spirit. Sorrow and suffering were her first teachers, <br /> the only possible ones for a woman otherwise unteachable.</i><br /> (p 270)<br /><br /><br />Unlike modern biographies and histories, there are no footnotes to this text nor is there a bibliography. Zweig does provide an endnote as to how he made the decision as to which letters to consider valid and which to consider fraudulent. Zweig also spends considerable time and effort on analyzing the psychology of the times and persons involved. He did live in the the time of, and was acquainted with, Freud. Whether this influenced his text is left to the reader to decide.<br /><br />As for myself, I have decided that I shall label Zweig's work an "interpretive" biography. He does build on sources that cannot be checked but he also provides an extremely readable history of the French Revolution from the royals' point of view, culminating with the death of Marie Antoinette. I recommend it with the caveat that it be combined with other historical works. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1267111406282120295" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer5671744496843425264">This book is the most complete biography of Marie Antoinette I have ever read.<br/><br/>First at all, the author made a huge research work in order to bring the historical facts which surrounded the Marie Antoinette's life.<br/><br/>In the first part of the book, Antoinette is described as a young monarch which her main concern, once she married Louis XVI, is to enjoy life as much as possible. In the meantime, <a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_of_Habsburg' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'>the Habsburgs </a> are trying to guide her destiny specially when she became Queen of France. Due to Louis' im</span>
  <span id="freeText5671744496843425264" style="display:none">This book is the most complete biography of Marie Antoinette I have ever read.<br /><br />First at all, the author made a huge research work in order to bring the historical facts which surrounded the Marie Antoinette's life.<br /><br />In the first part of the book, Antoinette is described as a young monarch which her main concern, once she married Louis XVI, is to enjoy life as much as possible. In the meantime, <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/House_of_Habsburg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">the Habsburgs </a> are trying to guide her destiny specially when she became Queen of France. Due to Louis' impotence in the first years of their marriage, her brother Joseph acted as a counselor in order to save the alliance with France.<br /><br />The <a href="https://www.goodreads.com" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">affair of the diamond necklace</a> was a real disaster to the queen's reputation. Alexandre Dumas in his <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1053073.The_Queen_s_Necklace" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">The Queen's Necklace</a> portrayed in full detail this scandal in the court of Louis XVI.<br /><br />With this affair, the queen credibility was put in charge thus provoking even more the hate of the French people.<br /><br />This historical fact is responsible for the advent of <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_revolution" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">the French Revolution</a>.<br /><br />In the meantime, the book also describes her love affair with the Swede <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axel_von_Fersen,_Jr." target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Count Axel von Fersen</a> who played an important role in the attempt to escape from Paris but then the royal family was captured in Varennes. A movie <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0084423/?ref_=nv_sr_1" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">La nuit de Varennes (1982)</a>, directed by Ettore Scola with Jean-Louis Barrault, Marcello Mastroianni, Hanna Schygulla was made based on this escape to Varennes.<br /><br />In the following pages, the author describes the execution of Louis XVI and the imprisonment of Marie Antoinette in the Conciergerie. Her execution is also described and until now, nobody knows where she is buried.<br /><br />Other books related to Marie Antoinette's life: <br /><br />4* <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/17157.Marie_Antoinette" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Marie Antoinette: The Journey</a> by Antonia Fraser which also have a movie version: <a href="http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0422720/?ref_=nv_sr_1" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Marie Antoinette (2006)</a> directed by Sofia Coppola, with Kirsten Dunst, Jason Schwartzman, Rip Torn.<br /><br />2* <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/7662230-the-hidden-diary-of-marie-antoinette" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">The Hidden Diary of Marie Antoinette</a> by Carolly Erickson.<br /><br />4* <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/415552.Abundance_A_Novel_of_Marie_Antoinette" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Abundance, A Novel of Marie Antoinette</a> by Sena Jeter Naslund.<br /><br />2* <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1916720.Les_larmes_de_Marie_Antoinette" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Les larmes de Marie-Antoinette</a> by Juliette Benzoni. A historical mystery book.<br /><br />3* <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1640919.Pintora_De_La_Reina_La_La_Favorita_De_Maria_Antonieta" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Pintora de la Reina, la Favorita de maria Antoineta</a> by Geneviève Chauvel. The life of Elisabeth Vigée Le Brun (1755-1842) who painted the most famous paintings of Marie Antoinette's family.<br /><br />4* <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1053073.The_Queen_s_Necklace" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">The Queen's Necklace</a> by Alexandre Dumas. The fiction version of the affair of the diamond necklace.<br /></span>

<div id="review_1162017689" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1162017689" />
    <a title="Jill" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1788674-jill"><img alt="Jill" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1432902126p2/1788674.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1162017689?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jill" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jill" href="/user/show/1788674-jill">Jill</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9782253146698" href="/book/show/1320141.Marie_Antoinette">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1788674-jill?shelf=german">german</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1788674-jill?shelf=good-writing">good-writing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1788674-jill?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1788674-jill?shelf=fran%C3%A7ais">français</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1788674-jill?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1788674-jill?shelf=2015-favorites">2015-favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1162017689" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18398273484490203354">What is the sum of a life? The manner in which an individual toiled away the majority of her living hours, the way history chooses to remember her, or how she approaches her final days, trying to live while knowing all that awaits her is death? In evaluating the sum of Marie-Antoinette’s life, the answer to this question is critical. Her rich story has been combed over by many a magnifying glass both during and after her reign as Queen of France, and it’s so full of anomalies that more than two </span>
  <span id="freeText18398273484490203354" style="display:none">What is the sum of a life? The manner in which an individual toiled away the majority of her living hours, the way history chooses to remember her, or how she approaches her final days, trying to live while knowing all that awaits her is death? In evaluating the sum of Marie-Antoinette’s life, the answer to this question is critical. Her rich story has been combed over by many a magnifying glass both during and after her reign as Queen of France, and it’s so full of anomalies that more than two centuries later, it’s still impossible to fully appreciate this historical figure, icon of an era, both vixen and victim. Stefan Zweig, however, in this seminal biography, does his best. In a way he seems to acknowledge the impossibility of it by noting the existence of several distinct Marie-Antoinette’s, one for each distinct epoch of history she occupied in her abridged life of 37 years. <br /><br />He begins with Marie-Antoinette’s voyage to France at the age of 14 to unite the feuding Bourbon and Habsburg crowns. Married to the impotent, indecisive, and altogether unroyal dauphin—soon-to-be Louis XVI, who will keep the most hilarious personal journal of all-time: his entry on July 14, 1789, the day the Parisians take the Bastille and kick off the Revolution reads, “Rien,” that is, “Nothing”—Marie-Antoinette tries to lose herself in a world of superficiality. Gambling, dances, dresses, and theatre occupy her first decade in France. She is the symbol of the era of rococo, the brilliant zenith of an age of luxury and carelessness birthed by Louis XIV. From Versailles she idles away her days, much to the chagrin of her mother, Empress Marie-Thérèse of Austria, who pleads her daughter for political seriousness. Marie-Antoinette responds, “Que me veut-elle? J’ai peur de m’ennuyer,” her words a motto for the era (“What does she want from me? I’m scared of being bored.”). <br /><br />But the tableau darkens. For Marie-Antoinette, home, that is, Versailles, will soon become a second-rate palace in Paris, which will become a guarded tower in prison, which will become a single jail cell, which will become an unmarked grave. She doesn’t know it, but we and Stefan Zweig do, which makes her various inadequacies in the years leading to the Revolution, evinced in fantastically entertaining affairs like “L’affaire du collier,” where she’s blamed for the theft of a diamond necklace, so vexing. A girl who does everything to eschew seriousness will be killed for it. And we read on…<br /><br />Finally, Marie-Antoinette realizes her mistakes and, late in the game, seems ready to play. The rules have changed, however. Blood and terror rule in Paris. The Queen of France is now “Madame Déficit.” It’s even a greater shame, then, that right when Marie-Antoinette finds herself a meaningful life with two children and a Swedish lover, her life is certainly over. <br /><br />Zweig details this extreme love story and the incredible inner strength Marie-Antoinette demonstrates until her final step on the platform of the guillotine with extraordinary psychological and personal detail. Unlike other biographers, Zweig does not content himself in reciting facts; he is there to unpack and repackage this ill-fated queen’s very essence. His approach is somewhat suspect, based both on personal correspondence discovered in the Austrian archives and the records of her Swedish lover and the Freudian insights popular in Zweig’s time. But it’s so carefully researched, so <i>lovingly</i> researched, that it feels infallible. <br /><br />Lately I’ve been wondering where humans find strength to survive in trying times. Where Marie-Antoinette found hers is a mystery: probably a combination between love for her family and friends and an ingrained, inborn belief that she was divinely royal. Has there ever been such a life? Such a magnificent story? Here Zweig recounts the life of a relic, the last and most gleaming example of an era, left to rot, headless, in an unmarked grave. But what Marie-Antoinette gives us, those who ponder her fate centuries later, is the possibility to remember her, marvel over her, and adopt some of the fortitude and wisdom with which she faced her final days. </span>
  <a data-text-id="18398273484490203354" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

For me, it is the most poignant biography of this figure which we associate with the revolution and the frivolity that led to it but yet had a rich interior life and deserved a different fate than that which life handed her.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer13826898003233762799">I've always steered clear of biographies on the premise that 400+ pages about any one person must be overkill. My dad strongly recommended this one, though, and I thought I'd give it a try. Much to my (pleasant) surprise, this book kept me interested on every page and through every chapter. Marie Antoinette's life history and the context both of her marriage to Louis XVI and the French Revolution are fascinating. It's not light reading, but it's certainly worth the effort. I really enjoyed it.</span>

          </span>

Mr. Zweig (an Austrian, like Marie Antoinette) wrote this book in the 40's using data from scores of letters (among other historical documents).   <span id="freeText6593537591985165360" style="display:none">This book was a very slow read BUT very interesing! Mr. Zweig (an Austrian, like Marie Antoinette) wrote this book in the 40's using data from scores of letters (among other historical documents). I read this book a few years after Marie Antoinette, The Journey, and I learned a great deal more about the life of Marie Antoinette from it! Zweig goes into great detail about many historical events involving the last Queen of France - from the extravagance of her early years to the sparse humiliating years during her imprisonment in the Conciergerie in Paris. Even though this book was a bit of a slow read, I'm giving it 5 stars because of the massive amount of detailed information the writer gives the reader about one of the most interesting women in history.<br /><br />I highly recommend reading this book and then going to Paris to see The Chateau de Versailles, the Domain de Marie Antoinette at Versailles, and the Conciergerie (her prison) in Paris! </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2795557241" />
    <a title="Lydia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2384784-lydia"><img alt="Lydia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1509295613p2/2384784.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lydia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lydia" href="/user/show/2384784-lydia">Lydia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/6675946-marie-antoinette">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2384784-lydia?shelf=biography">biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2384784-lydia?shelf=political">political</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2384784-lydia?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2384784-lydia?shelf=movie">movie</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2384784-lydia?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer2795557241&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2795557241" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5858270492556102517">As I stated when on What Are You Reading and Why bulletin board, I found this biography in a basement that I was helping to clean out. I was surprised by the age of the book and the subject matter. I realized that I knew very little about Queen Marie Antoinette other than the high-school snippets: her supposed reaction of "let them eat cake" to the famine in France, to her public beheading on all sorts of trumped charges, other than the main one --- she was not a good ruler.<br/><br/>Since this book was w</span>
  <span id="freeText5858270492556102517" style="display:none">As I stated when on What Are You Reading and Why bulletin board, I found this biography in a basement that I was helping to clean out. I was surprised by the age of the book and the subject matter. I realized that I knew very little about Queen Marie Antoinette other than the high-school snippets: her supposed reaction of "let them eat cake" to the famine in France, to her public beheading on all sorts of trumped charges, other than the main one --- she was not a good ruler.<br /><br />Since this book was written by her fellow countrymen {she was from Austria}, I thought at first that Zweig was not very sympathetic, but the further that I got into the book, he was writing as he interpreted the facts. <br /><br />Marie Antoinette was a teenager who was used as barter in an arranged marriage by her very shrewd mother, Empress Maria Theresa of Austria. Her mother tried to make it clear to Antoinette that although, she was marrying her into a great position, she also had political work to do while queen of France. Being immature, willful and a lousy student, the motherly advice was largely ignored, as was advice from her mother's experienced advisors. If Maria Theresa knew things about the French court and Antoinette's future husband, the Dauphin Louis XVI, she either failed to relay them or she did not explain them in the language that her daughter understood. The main points to me were that Versailles was basically a closed society, as members of the royal family seemed to have very little contact with their subjects and that the Dauphin was of limited intellectual capacity, impotent, seemingly of no personality, therefore a very insular individual due to his limits.<br /><br />Antoinette arrived at the court in a great position --- the future Queen. Not knowing the players in the court of Louis XV, the king's sisters made every attempt to manipulate her attitudes and others, did all that they could too ingratiate themselves to the future queen. The king and the dauphin, knowing his physical and intellectual shortcomings, allowed Antoinette more or less free rein. In addition, once becoming queen, she made disastrous political appointments.<br /><br />The author stated that she was queen of the Rocco, since that there seemed to be no limit to frivolous activities that Antoinette partook --- parties, nights at the Paris Opera {the only times that she went into the city} dresses, jewelry etc. All the while, Maria Theresa, before she passed, tried to remind Antoinette of the reasons that marriage was arranged. <br /><br />It wasn't until she had children that she realized her role as Queen and the true inadequacies of her husband as an autocratic ruler. When she and Louis XVI mounted the throne, they had no policies in place other than maintain the status quo, which the people would no longer tolerate. By this time, it was actually too late. Those who lived outside of the closed society of Versailles, the Third Estate/common citizens, were at this time fed up with war, the taxes, especially the salt tax, the church and the nobles. The country was ripe for a revolution and in need individual to blame. I think that Marie Antoinette got vilified because she was the only one seemingly with the intellect to run the country and the country was in ruins.<br /><br />There was always speculation that the queen had an affair. With Louis XVI as a husband, who can blame her. An affair before the Louis's corrective surgery might have been disastrous, but after she gave birth I could certainly understand it. Antoinette seemed to be a woman of physical needs so her affair with Count von Fresen seems reasonable. <br /><br />For followers of French history, there is not more to say other than what has been written about the King's failures as a ruler, the attempted fleeing of Paris, etc. I will say this --- the author stated that Louis sometimes read the history of Charles of England, who was beheaded. As I believe that Charles had trouble with Parliament and the king's place in the rule of the country. Marie Antoinette and Louis XVI were taught that they were ordained by God and blood to rule as autocrats {blood runs red for autocrats, too}. Fast forward some 125 years later in Russia --- a dimwitted Czar, a weak-witted, strong willed Czarina making political decisions, a church teaching obedience, a war and hunger. The results are the same.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5858270492556102517" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2110163637" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2110163637" />
    <a title="Lauralee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22943028-lauralee"><img alt="Lauralee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1527895031p2/22943028.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2110163637?book_show_action=true">Aug 31, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lauralee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lauralee" href="/user/show/22943028-lauralee">Lauralee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" " href="/book/show/21538591-marie-antoinette">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22943028-lauralee?shelf=biographies">biographies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22943028-lauralee?shelf=18th-century">18th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22943028-lauralee?shelf=france">france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22943028-lauralee?shelf=royalty">royalty</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2110163637" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4002774218851334613">Marie Antoinette is one of history’s most famous queens. Yet, Stefan Zweig’s classic biography of this queen tells us that if it was not for the French Revolution Marie Antoinette would most likely be forgotten in history. She would have been like many other French queens that came before her. Stefan Zweig also states that Marie Antoinette was neither a great woman nor a woman who deserved to be hated by the Revolution. Instead, Marie Antoinette was just an average woman who was thrust into the </span>
  <span id="freeText4002774218851334613" style="display:none">Marie Antoinette is one of history’s most famous queens. Yet, Stefan Zweig’s classic biography of this queen tells us that if it was not for the French Revolution Marie Antoinette would most likely be forgotten in history. She would have been like many other French queens that came before her. Stefan Zweig also states that Marie Antoinette was neither a great woman nor a woman who deserved to be hated by the Revolution. Instead, Marie Antoinette was just an average woman who was thrust into the spotlight that the French Revolution cast upon her.<br /><br /> It is obvious that Stefan Zweig had no love for his subject. He portrays her faults and is very critical of her personality. Marie Antoinette is often portrayed as vain, selfish, and a scatterbrain. She enjoys dancing, card games, and plays. She was pretty, charming, and pleasure-seeking. He states that she was always in her own little circle of friends and never reached out to the aristocracy or the poor. This caused her dislike among the aristocracy and the poor. When the time came for the French Revolution. no one wanted to help her. In the French Revolution, she becomes heavily involved in politics and has good diplomacy skills. Despite her adept skills in politics, she was too late. Stefan Zweig has also portrayed Marie Antoinette as haughty. Yet, in the French Revolution, her haughtiness becomes her charm and her strength.<br /><br /> Overall, this was a very detailed biography on Marie Antoinette. While it is a biased biography, it gave me some insight into the people in Marie Antoinette’s life, especially her enemies. I got a better sense of which of her enemies were secret royalists and which were not. Even though Stefan Zweig wrote this biography in the 1930s, it is still mostly accurate. Most of the questions he debates about Marie Antoinette are still being discussed today. It is also very thorough and answers many questions about her life. The writing was very witty, engaging, and humorous at times. I found myself laughing at a couple of his passages. Therefore, Marie Antoinette: The Portrait of an Average Woman reads like a novel. Even though there were a few ideas about Marie Antoinette that I disagreed with, there was a lot of information to be enjoyed. Thus, I recommend this for any Marie Antoinette enthusiast. While Stefan Zweig’s description of Marie Antoinette can be off-putting for of her sympathizers, it is still a worthy read. You can still see why Marie Antoinette: The Portrait of an Average Woman is a beloved classic.<br />(Note: I read an ARC copy of this book in courtesy of Edelweiss.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="4002774218851334613" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_454278874" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/454278874" />
    <a title="Gianna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12967326-gianna"><img alt="Gianna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1352217313p2/12967326.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/454278874?book_show_action=true">Nov 12, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gianna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gianna" href="/user/show/12967326-gianna">Gianna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12967326-gianna?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer454278874" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2898921594866761270">A recent proliferation of novels has romanticized the image of Marie Antoinette. Writers have focused on the wide-eye innocence of her childhood, her early days as Queen and, in general, have glamorized her person. Surrounded by the veil of myths, touched by the force of a very real drama, Marie Antoinette remains the most popular queen of France and perhaps even Europe. Zweig’s biography doesn’t romanticize or glamorize Marie Antoinette. In fact, he states clearly in the Introduction that Marie</span>
  <span id="freeText2898921594866761270" style="display:none">A recent proliferation of novels has romanticized the image of Marie Antoinette. Writers have focused on the wide-eye innocence of her childhood, her early days as Queen and, in general, have glamorized her person. Surrounded by the veil of myths, touched by the force of a very real drama, Marie Antoinette remains the most popular queen of France and perhaps even Europe. Zweig’s biography doesn’t romanticize or glamorize Marie Antoinette. In fact, he states clearly in the Introduction that Marie Antoinette was a mediocre woman, who, by virtue of her royal birth, marriage, and a brutal end, achieved the status of immortality: “The consciousness of a supreme duty lifted her character to a higher level than she had ever known. Just before the mortal, the transient frame perished, the immortal work of art was perfected” (p. VI). <br /><br />Young Marie Antoinette is described as carefree and her character as full of “levity.” She lived only for her own amusement, and as Queen, chose to frequent the Opera House, but never visited a hospital or entered a middle-class home. It is not that Zweig doesn’t like who Marie Antoinette was (he doesn’t hide his disparaging feelings, for example, for the aristocratic faction that betrays her). Rather, he depicts her as a typical product of her era. Marie Antoinette was not just the Queen of France; she was the “Queen of Rococo.” <br /><br />Zweig rejects some popular and frequently cited anecdotes as myths. For instance, he claims it is unlikely that the young royal couple exclaimed, “Lord God, guide us and protect us, for we are too young to rule,” as Madam Campan notes in her memoires. I have to stress that I am not a historian, so I cannot judge the scholarship of Zweig’s work, but the book seems well-researched and supported with rich arguments. Overall, I enjoyed reading it and would recommend it to anyone interested in the era and Marie Antoinette’s evolution from a young, naïve woman to the symbol she has become. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2898921594866761270" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review325683098">

Also, the inability of Louis XVI to take a single decision seems responsible for many of its failures. A good read, though a bit long at times</span>

<div id="review_1799449366" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1799449366" />
    <a title="Richard Thompson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11657479-richard-thompson"><img alt="Richard Thompson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530711053p2/11657479.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1799449366?book_show_action=true">Oct 31, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Richard Thompson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Richard Thompson" href="/user/show/11657479-richard-thompson">Richard Thompson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11657479-richard-thompson?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11657479-richard-thompson?shelf=biography">biography</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1799449366" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16599744561954716009">I have read several books recently that either espoused or rejected the "great man" theory of history -- that history is the story of the deeds of great men in important positions. On one end of the spectrum is the execrable "Why The West Rules For Now," in which the author kept insisting that history is about "maps not chaps." Zweig sits at the other end of the spectrum, seemingly taking the position here that history is the the story of the personal psychologies of mediocre people who are thru</span>
  <span id="freeText16599744561954716009" style="display:none">I have read several books recently that either espoused or rejected the "great man" theory of history -- that history is the story of the deeds of great men in important positions. On one end of the spectrum is the execrable "Why The West Rules For Now," in which the author kept insisting that history is about "maps not chaps." Zweig sits at the other end of the spectrum, seemingly taking the position here that history is the the story of the personal psychologies of mediocre people who are thrust by unfolding events into extraordinary positions. So for example, we see the French Revolution as arising out of the ineffectiveness of Louis XVI, which was caused in turn according to Zweig by the king's sexual disfunction. And the revolution is kept on course by a minor official who happens to smell a rat when the royal family attempts their ill fated flight that ended in Varennes. I do believe that much of history is driven by individual action and coincidence and that more than half of the fun of studying history comes from the stories of individuals and their choices. Zweig takes it a little further than I might, but when the form of the book is a biography, his historical point of view is perhaps appropriate. So I thoroughly enjoyed his portrait of Marie Antoinette as a mediocrity who spent the first years of her reign in pursuit of pleasure and who managed to rise to a level of dignity, although not quite greatness, under the relentless harassment of the popular press and the revolution that eventually led to her execution. It is a fascinating story, and Zweig tells it in a rich literary style that brings the characters and events brilliantly to light.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16599744561954716009" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/995525649" />
    <a title="Inessa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8368198-inessa"><img alt="Inessa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1356617085p2/8368198.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/995525649?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Inessa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Inessa" href="/user/show/8368198-inessa">Inessa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer995525649" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2896303294604340053">Psychological analysis of historical figures makes this book interesting to read. description of french revolution and fates/destinies of its biggest players, make you draw parallels to more recent events in Russia, crimes committed by leaders of the socialist revolution and its ultimate failure, people corruption, etc. Relevant book.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sophiemaile_fischersesd.Org" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sophiemaile_fischersesd.Org" href="/user/show/47328103-sophiemaile-fischersesd-org">Sophiemaile_fischersesd.Org</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47328103-sophiemaile-fischersesd-org?shelf=awesome">awesome</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/47328103-sophiemaile-fischersesd-org?shelf=loved">loved</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1561569624" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12779111123676831035">This book is an adult book, and is delightful, telling all the twists and turns, the fear and happiness that Marie Antoinette experienced. It is well written, and I give it 5 stars! I never thought of Marie Antoinette this way, I always thought she was the lady who yelled, "Let them eat cake!" and then died on the guillotine. But she's not. She's much more complex. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2759739967" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9000334977031833573">Excellent book, love the writing. The Afterword was particularly good and perhaps one should consider reading first? Although I have read extensively about the revolution, this is my first book specifically about Marie Antoinette and one gains a new appreciation of what she experienced during her life. This is an unjudgemental account of an average woman who had an extraordinary life.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('oLzV52yhblLkHJW8JPTWZZd+YmLJ2IJPmAr8qGe1LzNhdmXTCayLhJAjHqwYBmFYVP2cq2ybvkG4LEazquEInQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('9zHNnxwcqo40zQ1pdKm7Jvb0P8Z5ZWi317tDDt1aw3Y2+32reRFPWEDyhnlIWwwbNXfBD9wmVLn3nfkVEA7k2A==')})
  }

Il semble redécouvrir le monde à chaque tour. Les ingénieurs de Google ont réussi à calculer la durée maximale de son attention : 8 secondes. Ces mêmes ingénieurs ont évalué la durée d’attention de la génération des millenials, celle qui a grandi avec les écrans connectés : 9 secondes. Nous sommes devenus des poissons rouges, enfermés dans le bocal de nos écrans, soumis au manège de nos alertes et de nos messages instantanés.\nUne étude du Journal of Social and Clinical Psychology évalue à 30 minutes le temps maximum d’exposition aux réseaux sociaux et aux écrans d’Internet au-delà duquel apparaît une menace pour la santé mentale. D’après cette étude, mon cas est désespéré, tant ma pratique quotidienne est celle d’une dépendance aux signaux qui encombrent l’écran de mon téléphone. Nous sommes tous sur le chemin de l’addiction : enfants, jeunes, adultes.\nPour ceux qui ont cru à l’utopie numérique, dont je fais partie, le temps des regrets est arrivé. Ainsi de Tim Berners Lee, « l’inventeur » du web, qui essaie de désormais de créer un contre-Internet pour annihiler sa création première. L’utopie, pourtant, était belle, qui rassemblait, en une communion identique, adeptes de Teilhard de Chardin ou libertaires californiens sous acide.\nLa servitude numérique est le modèle qu’ont construit les nouveaux empires, sans l’avoir prévu, mais avec une détermination implacable. Au cœur du réacteur, nul déterminisme technologique, mais un projet qui traduit la mutation d’un nouveau capitaliste : l’économie de l’attention. Il s’agit d’augmenter la productivité du temps pour en extraire encore plus de valeur. Après avoir réduit l’espace, il s’agit d’étendre le temps tout en le comprimant, et de créer un instantané infini. L’accélération générale a remplacé l’habitude par l’attention, et la satisfaction par l’addiction. Et les algorithmes sont aujourd’hui les machines-outils de cette économie…\nCette économie de l’attention détruit, peu à peu, nos repères. Notre rapport aux médias, à l’espace public, au savoir, à la vérité, à l’information, rien n’échappe à l’économie de l’attention qui préfère les réflexes à la réflexion et les passions à la raison. Les lumières philosophiques s’éteignent au profit des signaux numériques. Le marché de l’attention, c’est la société de la fatigue.\nLes regrets, toutefois, ne servent à rien. Le temps du combat est arrivé, non pas pour rejeter la civilisation numérique, mais pour en transformer la nature économique et en faire un projet qui abandonne le cauchemar transhumaniste pour Cette expérience se prolonge à l\'école, au cours de danse, à la bibliothèque municipale, partout où le langage impose la position dominante du genre masculin : « Garce. Le mot revient et la hante. Mais il a des pouvoirs. » Dans ce roman d\'une puissance exceptionnelle, Camille Laurens déploie le destin d\'une femme confrontée aux mutations de la société française de ces quarante dernières années. La narratrice emporte dans sa voix les grandes problématiques de l\'éducation des femmes, de la domination masculine et de la transmission des valeurs féministes aux jeunes générations. Le parcours de Laurence Barraqué se fait la chambre d\'échos de toutes celles qui furent élevées dans l\'idée d\'une supériorité des hommes. L\'auteur saisit avec acuité les moments charnières de l\'enfance au cours desquels se joue l\'adulte que l\'on va devenir. //<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_24683905'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24683905-berezina?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Berezina<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/810151.Sylvain_Tesson?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Sylvain Tesson<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p6\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p0\"><\/span><\/span> 3.88 avg rating &mdash; 661 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 2015\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer2457412352269806993\">En octobre 1812, piégé dans Moscou en flammes, Napoléon replie la Grande Armée vers la France. Commence La retraite de Russie, l\'une des plus tragique épopée de l\'Histoire humaine. La Retraite est une course à La mort, une marche des fous, une échapp<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText2457412352269806993\" style=\"display:none\">En octobre 1812, piégé dans Moscou en flammes, Napoléon replie la Grande Armée vers la France. Commence La retraite de Russie, l\'une des plus tragique épopée de l\'Histoire humaine. La Retraite est une course à La mort, une marche des fous, une échappée d\'enfer. Deux cents ans plus tard, je décide de répéter l\'itinéraire de l\'armée agonisante, de ces cavaliers désarçonnés, de ces fantassins squelettiques, de ces hommes à plumets qui avaient préjugé de l\'invincibilité de l\'Aigle. Il ne s\'agit pas d\'une commémoration (commémore-t-on l\'horreur ?), encore moins d\'une célébration, il s\'agit de saluer par-delà les siècles et les verstes, ces Français de l\'an XII aveuglés par le soleil corse et fracassés sur les récifs du cauchemar. Le géographe Cédric Gras, le photographe Thomas Goisque et deux amis russes, Vassili et Vitaly, sont de la partie. Pour l\'aventure, nous enfourchons des side-cars soviétiques de marque Oural. Ces motocyclettes redéfinissent en permanence les lois élémentaires de la mécanique. Rien ne saurait les arrêter (pas même leurs freins). Notre escouade se compose de trois Oural, chargées ras la gueule de pièces détachées et de livres d\'Histoire. Au long de quatre mille kilomètres, en plein hiver, nous allons dérouler le fil de la mémoire entre Moscou et Paris où l\'Empereur arrivera le 15 XII 1812, laissant derrière lui son armée en lambeaux. Le jour, les mains luisantes de cambouis, nous lisons les Mémoires du général de Caulaincourt. Le soir, nous nous assommons de vodka pour éloigner les fantômes. A l\'aube, nous remettons les gaz vers une nouvelle étape du chemin de croix. Smolensk, Minsk, Berezina, Vilnius : les stèles de la souffrance défilent à cinquante à l\'heure. Partout, nous rencontrons des Russes qui ne tiennent aucune rigueur à l\'Empereur à bicorne. Sous nos casques crénelés de stalactites, nous prenons la mesure des tourments des soldats et nous menons grand train ce débat intérieur : Napoléon était-il un antéchrist qui précipita l\'Europe dans l\'abîme ou bien un visionnaire génial dont le seul tort fut de croire qu\'il suffisait de vouloir pour triompher, et que les contingences se pliaient toujours aux rêves ? Mais très vite, nous devons abandonner ces questions métaphysiques car un cylindre vient de rendre l\'âme, la nuit tombe sur la Biélorussie et trois foutus camions polonais sont déjà en travers de la route.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"2457412352269806993\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return false;\">...more<\/a>\n\n    <\/div>\n\n      <div class=\'wtrButtonContainer       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/25573.Stefan_Zweig">About Stefan Zweig</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/25573.Stefan_Zweig"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1190140738p3/25573.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/25573.Stefan_Zweig">Stefan Zweig
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
5,937
followers
</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11190742570712407261">Stefan Zweig was one of the world's most famous writers during the 1920s and 1930s, especially in the U.S., South America, and Europe. He produced novels, plays, biographies, and journalist pieces. Among his most famous works are <i>Beware of Pity</i>, <i>Letter from an Unknown Woman</i>, and <i>Mary, Queen of Scotland and the Isles</i>. He and his second wife committed suicide in 1942.<br/><br/>Zweig studied in Austria, France</span>
  <span id="freeText11190742570712407261" style="display:none">Stefan Zweig was one of the world's most famous writers during the 1920s and 1930s, especially in the U.S., South America, and Europe. He produced novels, plays, biographies, and journalist pieces. Among his most famous works are <i>Beware of Pity</i>, <i>Letter from an Unknown Woman</i>, and <i>Mary, Queen of Scotland and the Isles</i>. He and his second wife committed suicide in 1942.<br /><br />Zweig studied in Austria, France, and Germany before settling in Salzburg in 1913. In 1934, driven into exile by the Nazis, he emigrated to England and then, in 1940, to Brazil by way of New York. Finding only growing loneliness and disillusionment in their new surroundings, he and his second wife committed suicide.<br /><br />Zweig's interest in psychology and the teachings of Sigmund Freud led to his most characteristic work, the subtle portrayal of character. Zweig's essays include studies of Honoré de Balzac, Charles Dickens, and Fyodor Dostoyevsky (<i>Drei Meister</i>, 1920; <i>Three Masters</i>) and of Friedrich Hölderlin, Heinrich von Kleist, and Friedrich Nietzsche (<i>Der Kampf mit dem Dämon</i>, 1925; <i>Master Builders</i>). He achieved popularity with <i>Sternstunden der Menschheit</i> (1928; <i>The Tide of Fortune</i>), five historical portraits in miniature. He wrote full-scale, intuitive rather than objective, biographies of the French statesman Joseph Fouché (1929), Mary Stuart (1935), and others. His stories include those in <i>Verwirrung der Gefühle</i> (1925; <i>Conflicts</i>). He also wrote a psychological novel, <i>Ungeduld des Herzens</i> (1938; <i>Beware of Pity</i>), and translated works of Charles Baudelaire, Paul Verlaine, and Emile Verhaeren.<br /><br />Most recently, his works provided the inspiration for 2014 film <i>The Grand Budapest Hotel</i>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11190742570712407261" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

She simply accepted the rights of her royal position and performed no duties in exchange. She wanted to combine two things which are, in actual human experience, incompatible; she wanted to reign and at the same time to enjoy.&rdquo;</span>
